Usurpation Possession Substitutes for Sale Possession
States the principle that usurpation possession substitutes for sale possession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that usurpation possession substitutes for sale possession and apply it to determine when a buyer is considered in possession after purchasing an item he previously took without right.

Core Idea
Usurpation possession (ghasb) carries liability just like sale possession—so prior ghasb suffices as sale possession.

In this lesson, we examine a principle from al-Hidayah that resolves a practical dilemma: if someone unlawfully takes an item and later purchases it, does he need to physically repossess it? The Hanafi answer is no, based on a powerful analogy (qiyas) between usurpation and sale possession, both of which impose liability (daman) for loss. This principle streamlines transactions by eliminating redundant physical possession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Principle
Read the Arabic phrase: 'قَبْضُ الْعَصَبِ يَنُوبُ عَنْ قَبْضِ الْبَيْعِ' (qabd al-ghasb yanubu 'an qabd al-bay'). Understand its literal meaning: usurpation possession stands in place of sale possession.
Note: 'العصَب' is likely a scribal variant of 'الغصب' (usurpation).
2
Understand the Basis: Shared Liability
Both usurpation possession and sale possession create liability (daman) if the item is destroyed. This common characteristic makes them legally similar (mutamathil).
Daman ensures compensation; trust possession (amana) does not carry liability.
3
Apply the Qiyas (Analogy)
The ruling from the original case (asl: qabd al-ghasb creates liability) is transferred to the new case (far': qabd al-bay' also creates liability) because the effective cause ('illa) is the same: the possessor bears the risk of loss.
This is qiyas jaliy (clear analogy).
4
Distinguish Intent and Witnesses
The application depends on the buyer's intention. If he took the item for himself, he becomes possessor immediately upon contract by agreement. If he took it to return to the seller, he must not have witnessed his intention; otherwise, his possession is a trust (amana) and does not substitute.
Witnesses transform the possession into a trust, which lacks liability.
5
Test the Application: The Runaway Slave Case
Consider a buyer who takes a runaway slave without witnesses, intending to return him. Later he buys the slave. According to the principle, his prior usurpation possession suffices as sale possession, so he does not need to repossess.
Without witnesses, the possession is usurpation (ghasb), not trust.

Possession of usurpation stands in the place of possession of sale. (25) Qabd al-ghasb yanubu 'an qabd al-bay'. Translation: The possession of usurpation can substitute for the possession of sale. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that the usurper's possession is like the buyer's possession, because in both, if the sold item is destroyed, liability (daman) arises. Therefore, both are similar and strong, and one strong can stand in place of another strong one. The author of Hidayah, by mentioning this principle, is explaining that in the above case, if the buyer did not set up witnesses to return the slave to the seller at the time of taking him, then this possession is usurpation possession. Now if this possessor (the buyer) purchases this slave, he does not need a new possession; rather, just the contract makes him the possessor, because his first possession was usurpation possession, and usurpation possession can stand in the place of sale possession. So the buyer's first possession (usurpation) will stand in the place of the second possession (sale possession), and there is no need for a new possession. The runaway slave... and because it is not capable of delivery. The runaway slave that is not absolute, i.e., it is not a runaway in the right of one of the two contracting parties, its sale is permissible, like someone selling it to a man who claims it is with him, because the prohibited thing is the sale of the absolute runaway, and this is not a runaway in the right of the buyer, so the inability to deliver—which prevents permissibility—is negated. Then, does he become a possessor by the contract alone or not? If he took possession for himself, he becomes a possessor immediately after the purchase by agreement. And if he took possession to return (to the owner), then he either witnesses to that or not. If the first, he does not become a possessor because it is a trust (amana) with him, so if it perishes before reaching the master, it perishes from the master's property, and trust possession does not substitute for sale possession because the possession of liability (daman) is stronger due to its firmness in binding and ownership. (Al-‘Inayah: Kitab al-Buyu‘, Bab al-Bay‘ al-Fasid, vol. 6, p. 421). Al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Buyu‘, Bab al-Bay‘ al-Fasid, vol. 3, p. 56.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage presents a key Hanafi legal principle: possession through usurpation (qabd al-ghasb) can stand in place of possession through sale (qabd al-bay'). The commentary follows the order: first the principle is stated in Arabic, then its Urdu translation and explanation. The author of Hidayah uses this principle to resolve a specific case: if a buyer initially took a slave by force (to return him to the seller) without witnesses, that possession is considered usurpation (ghasb). Later, if the buyer purchases that slave, he does not need to take physical possession again because the prior usurpation possession already qualifies as a valid sale possession. The analogy is drawn: both usurpation and sale possession create liability (daman) for loss, so they are similar in strength and one can substitute for the other. The passage then quotes the Hidayah text discussing the sale of a runaway slave ('abd abiq). The key ruling is that if the buyer took the slave for himself, he becomes possessor immediately upon contract by agreement. If he took it to return, and he witnesses that, then it is a trust (amana) and does not substitute; but if he did not witness, the possession is usurpation (ghasb) and does substitute. The principle is from Zahir al-Riwayah as found in al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
قبض الغصب (قبض العصبة in text) (qabd al-ghasb): The act of taking possession of another's property without right, which in this context is considered a valid form of possession that can substitute for sale possession.
قبض البيع (qabd al-bay'): Possession taken as part of a valid sale contract, which completes the transfer of ownership and liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) He must take physical possession again after purchase.
B) His prior possession (usurpation) counts as valid sale possession.
C) The sale is invalid without new possession.
D) The slave remains in the seller's possession.

Key Takeaway
Usurpation possession that creates liability substitutes for sale possession; intention and witnessing determine whether possession is liability or trust.

Definition of Mal and Sale of the Runaway Slave
Defines mal as what can be preserved, then applies the ruling to sale of a runaway slave.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of mal (property) and apply it to determine the validity of selling a runaway slave.

Core Idea
Mal is what can be preserved; a runaway slave cannot be delivered, so its sale is forbidden unless uncertainty is removed.

This passage introduces the Hanafi definition of property (mal), a foundational concept for all transactions. It then applies this definition to a specific case: the sale of a runaway slave. Understanding this definition is crucial for distinguishing between valid and invalid sales based on deliverability and preservability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Definition of Mal
Locate the statement 'al-mal ma yumkin ihrazuhu' and note the two conditions: (1) it can be preserved, (2) it remains over two periods of time.
This is a legal definition, not a linguistic one.
2
Locate the Ruling on the Runaway Slave
Find the hadith and the statement 'la yajuz' (not permissible). Understand that the default ruling for an absolute runaway is prohibition.
The hadith is from al-Asl, a Zahir al-Riwayah text.
3
Examine the Exception
Identify when the sale is permissible: when the slave is not a runaway relative to one of the contracting parties, e.g., the buyer claims the slave is with him.
The prohibition is only for the absolute runaway (mutlaq).
4
Analyze the Possession Discussion
Understand the sub-issue: if the buyer takes possession (qabd) for himself or for return, and how different modes of possession affect liability.
Usurpation possession (ghasb) substitutes for sale possession, but trust possession does not.

Usul-e-Hidayah Vol. 3. (26) Property is that which can be preserved. Translation: Property is that which can be stored. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that in Shari‘ah, property is called that which can remain for two periods and can be preserved, and that which cannot be preserved is not called property. And the sale of the runaway slave—the sale of the absolute runaway is not permissible, due to what Muhammad mentioned in al-Asl, saying: 'It reached us from the Messenger of Allah (PBUH) that he forbade sale of gharar and sale of the runaway slave'—and because it is not capable of delivery. And the runaway that is not absolute, i.e., it is not a runaway in the right of one of the two contracting parties, its sale is permissible, like someone selling it to a man who claims it is with him, because the prohibited thing is the sale of the absolute runaway, and this is not a runaway in the right of the buyer, so the inability to deliver that prevents permissibility is negated. Then, does he become a possessor by the contract alone or not? If he took possession for himself, he becomes a possessor immediately after purchase by agreement. And if he took it to return, then he either witnesses to that or not. If the first, he does not become a possessor because it is a trust, so if it perishes before reaching the master, it perishes from the master's property, and trust possession does not substitute for sale possession because the possession of liability is stronger due to its firmness in binding and ownership. As for binding: because the buyer, if he refrains from taking possession of the sold item, is compelled to do so, and after possession, the seller has no right to cancel. (Al-‘Inayah: Kitab al-Buyu‘, Bab al-Bay‘ al-Fasid, vol. 6, p. 422). And if he did not witness at the time of taking—correctly at the time of possession—he must become a possessor because it is usurpation possession, and usurpation possession is liability possession, so it substitutes for sale possession, and it is liability possession. (Al-Binayah: Kitab al-Buyu‘, Bab al-Bay‘ al-Fasid, vol. 8, p. 163). Al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Buyu‘, Bab al-Bay‘ al-Fasid, vol. 3, p. 57.

Commentary
This passage first defines the term 'mal' (property) in Hanafi law: it is that which can be preserved (ihraz), meaning it can be stored and remain over time. The definition is foundational for many transactions. The passage then applies this to the sale of a runaway slave ('abd abiq). Citing a hadith from Imam Muhammad in al-Asl, the sale of an absolute runaway slave (one that is not known to be in anyone's possession) is forbidden due to gharar (uncertainty) and inability to deliver. However, if the slave is not a runaway relative to one of the parties—e.g., the buyer claims the slave is with him—then the sale is permissible because the inability to deliver is eliminated. The passage then returns to the issue of possession: if the buyer took the slave for himself, he becomes possessor upon contract. If he took it to return, and he witnessed that, it remains a trust and does not count as sale possession; but if he did not witness, it becomes usurpation possession (ghasb), which does count as sale possession. This reiterates the previous principle.

Key Terms
المال (al-mal): Anything that can be preserved (ihraz) and remains over two periods of time.
إحراز (ihraz): The act of preserving or securing property so it remains safe.
العبد الآبق (al-'abd al-abiq): A slave who has run away from his owner, making delivery uncertain.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) A house

C) A gold coin
D) A camel

Key Takeaway
Mal in Hanafi law is defined by preservability and durability; the sale of an undeliverable item (like a runaway slave) is invalid unless the uncertainty is removed.

Definition of Wealth and Its Application in Sale
States the Hanafi definition of wealth and its application to two sale cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of wealth (mal) and apply it to determine the permissibility of selling an absconding slave and the right of ascent.

Core Idea
Wealth is only what can be preserved; without preservation there is no valid sale.

The first principle in this passage defines wealth in Sharīʿah as anything that can be preserved and remains over time. This definition is critical because only wealth can be the subject of a valid sale. The author then applies it to two cases: the sale of an absconding slave and the sale of the right of ascent after a building collapse. Understanding this principle will help you distinguish between saleable property and non-saleable rights.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition of mal
Read the opening phrase: al-māl mā yumkin iḥrāzuhu. Understand that mal must be capable of preservation (iḥrāz) and durability (bāqī ʿalā zamanayn).
2
Apply the definition to the absconding slave case
The absolute absconder cannot be sold because delivery is impossible, making preservation moot. However, if the buyer knows the location, the sale is permissible because delivery is possible.
This is supported by hadith prohibiting gharar.
3
Apply the definition to the right of ascent case
After the upper floor collapses, only the right to climb remains; this is not wealth because it cannot be preserved, so its sale is invalid.
The right is in the air, not a tangible asset.
4
Note the hadith prohibition
The prohibition of selling an absconding slave is supported by a hadith reported by Muhammad in al-Asl.
The hadith forbids gharar and sale of absconding slave.
5
Distinguish between absolute and relative absconder
The text explains that if the slave is not an absconder from the buyer's perspective (e.g., buyer knows where he is), the sale is valid because delivery is possible.
This distinction is crucial for practical application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

ترجمہ: مال وہ ہوتا ہے جس کو محفوظ کرناممکن ہو۔
۵۶
تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ شرعاً مال وہ کہلاتا ہے جو دو زمانوں تک باقی رہ سکے اور اس کو محفوظ کرناممکن ہو اور جس کا محفوظ کرنا ممکن نہ ہو وہ مال نہیں کہلائے گا۔ وَلَا يَجُوزُ بَيْعُ الْآبِقِ بَيْعُ الْآبِقِ الْمُطْلَقِ لَا يَجُوزُ لِمَا ذَكَرَ مُحَمَّدٌ فِي الْأَصْلِ بِقَوْلِهِ بَلَغَنَا عَنْ رَسُولِ اللَّهِ صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ أَنَّهُ نَهَى عَنْ بَيْعِ الْغَرَرِ وَعَنْ بَيْعِ الْعَبْدِ الْآبِقِ وَلأَنَّهُ غَيْرُ مَقْدُورِ التَّسليم، وَالْآبِقُ الَّذِي لَا يَكُونُ مُطْلَقًا وَهُوَ الَّذِي لَا يَكُونُ آبِقًا فِي حَقِّ أَحَدِ الْمُتَعَاقِدَيْنِ جَازَ بَيْعُهُ كَمَنْ بَاعَهُ مِنْ رَجُلٍ يَزْعُمُ أَنَّهُ عِنْدَهُ لأَنَّ الْمَنْهِيَّ عَنْهُ بَيْعُ الْمُطْلَقِ مِنْهُ، وَهَذَا غَيْرُ آبِقِ فِي حَقِّ الْمُشْتَرِى فَيَنْتَفِي الْعَجْزُ عَنُ التَّسْلِيمِ الْمَائِعِ [؟] مِنَ الْجَوَازِ ثُمَّ هَلْ يَصِيرُ قَابِضًا بِمُجَرَّدِ الْعَقْدِ أَوْ لَا إِنْ كَانَ قَبَضَهُ لِنَفْسِهِ يَصِيرُ قَابِضًا عَقِبَ الشّرَاءِ بِالاتِّفَاقِ، وَإِنْ قَبَضَهُ لِلرَّدْ، فَإِمَّا أَنْ يَشْهَدَ عَلَى ذَلِكَ أَوْ لَا ، فَإِنْ كَانَ الْأَوَّلُ لَا يَصِيرُ قَابِضًا لِأَنَّهُ أَمَانَةٌ عِنْدَهُ، حَتَّى لَوْ هَلَكَ قَبْلَ الْوُصُولِ إِلَى الْمَوْلَى هَلَكَ مِنْ مَالِ الْمَوْلَى وَقَبْضُ الْأَمَانَةِ لَا يَنُوبُ عَنْ قَبْضِ الْبَيْعِ لِأَنَّ قَبْضَ الصَّمَانِ أَقْوَى لِتَأْكُدِهِ بِاللُّزُومِ وَالْمِلْكِ، أَمَّا اللُّزُومُ فَلَانَّ الْمُشْتَرِي لَوْ امْتَنَعَ عَنْ قَبْضِ الْمَبِيعِ أُجْبِرَ عَلَيْهِ وَبَعْدَ الْقَبْضِ لَيْسَ لِلْبَائِعِ فَسُخُهُ.
(العناية: كتاب البيوع، باب البيع الفاسد، ج ۶ ص (۴۲۲
ولو كان لم يشهد عند الأخذ) صوابه عند القبض ( يجب أن يصير قابضا لأنه قبض غصب وقبض الغصب قبض ضمان، فينوب عن قبض البيع وهو قبض ضمان. (البناية كتاب البيوع، باب البيع الفاسد، ج۸ ص ۱۶۳)
الهداية كتاب البيوع، باب البيع الفاسد، ج ۳ ص ۵۷

اصول ہدایہ ثالث
۵۷
صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلارہے ہیں کہ اگر دومنزل مکان میں ایک کی تحتانی منزل ہو اور دوسرے کی فوقانی ہو اور دونوں گر جائیں یا صرف فوقانی گر جائے، اب اگر وہ صرف فوقانی حصہ کے حق تعلی کو فروخت کرنا چاہے تو یہ جائز نہیں ہے، کیونکہ جب بالا خانہ گر گیا تو اب صرف اس کو تعلی یعنی اوپر چڑھنے کا حق حاصل ہے اور تعلی مال نہیں ہے کیونکہ اس کو محفوظ کرنا ممکن نہیں ہے، اور جو مال نہ ہو اس کو فروخت کرنا جائز نہیں ہے، لہذا صرف حق تعلی کو بھی فروخت نہیں کر سکتے ،حق تعلمی اس لیے مال نہیں ہے کہ اس میں احراز و جمع کا امکان نہیں ہے اور بیع کے لیے ضروری ہے کہ وہ مال ہو ” لأن البيع اسم لمبادلة المال بالمال
باب المرابحة والتولية
(۲۷) اَلشُّبُهَةُ كَالْحَقِيقَةِ فِي بَيْعِ الْمُرَابَحَةِ اِحْتِيَاطَاً.
ترجمہ: احتیاطاً بیع مرابحہ میں شبہ کو حقیقت کا درجہ دے دیا گیا ہے۔
(قَوْلُهُ وَعُلْوِ سَقَطَ أَى لَمْ يَجُزُ بَيْعُ عُلْمٍ بَعْدَ انْهِدَامِهِ لِأَنَّ الْبَاقِيَ بَعْدَ سُقُوطِهِ حَقُّ التَّعَلَّى، وَهُوَ لَيْسَ بِمَالٍ لَّانَّ الْمَالَ مَا يُمْكِنُ إِحْرَازُهُ، وَالْمَالُ هُوَ الْمَحَلُّ الْمَبِيعُ. (البحر الرائق: كتاب البيوع، باب البيع الفاسد، ج ۶ ص ۸۸) قَالَ فِي الْفَتْحِ وَإِذَا كَانَ السُّفُلُ لِرَجُلٍ وَعُلُوهُ لِآخَرَ فَسَقَطَا أَوْ سَقَطَ الْعُلُوُ وَحْدَهُ فَبَاعَ صَاحِبُ الْعُلْوِ عُلُوهُ لَمْ يَجُزُ ، لأنَّ الْمَسِيعَ حِينَئِذٍ لَيْسَ إِلَّا حَقُّ التَّعَلِّى، وَحَقُّ التَّعَلِّى لَيْسَ بِمَالٍ، لِأَنَّ الْمَالَ عَيْنْ يُمْكِنُ إِحْرَاؤُهَا وَإِمْسَاكُهَا وَلَا هُوَ حَقٌّ مُتَعَلِّقٌ بِالْمَالِ بَلْ هُوَ حَقٌّ مُتَعَلِّقٌ بِالْهَوَاءِ، وَلَيْسَ الْهَوَاءُ مَالًا يُبَاعُ وَالْمَسِيعُ لَا بُدَّ أَنْ يَكُونَ أَحَدَهُمَا. (رد المحتار: كتاب البيوع، باب البیع الفاسد، ج ۵ ص ۵۲)
الهداية: كتاب البيوع، باب المرابحة والتولية، ح٣ ص ٧٦
Wealth is what can be preserved. Translation: Wealth is that which can be preserved. 56 Explanation: The meaning of this principle is that, according to Shari'ah, wealth is called that which can remain for two durations and can be preserved, and that which cannot be preserved is not called wealth. The sale of an absconding slave is not permissible. The sale of the absolute absconder is not permissible, because Muhammad mentioned in al-Asl with his statement: It has reached us from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) that he forbade the sale of gharar and the sale of an absconding slave, and because it is not possible to deliver. And the absconding slave that is not absolute, i.e., he is not an absconder in the right of one of the two contracting parties, his sale is permissible, like one who sells him to a man who claims that the slave is with him, because what is prohibited is the sale of the absolute one, and this is not an absconder in the right of the buyer, so the inability to deliver, which prevents permissibility, is negated. Then: Does he become a possessor merely by the contract or not? If he took possession for himself, he becomes a possessor immediately after purchase by agreement. If he took possession for return, then either he witnesses that or not. If he witnesses, he does not become a possessor because it is a trust (amanah) with him, such that if it perishes before reaching the owner, it perishes from the owner's wealth, and possession of a trust does not substitute for possession of sale because possession of guarantee is stronger due to its being confirmed by obligation and ownership. As for obligation, if the buyer refrains from taking possession of the sold item, he is compelled to do so, and after possession the seller cannot rescind. (Al-'Inayah: Kitab al-Buyu', Bab al-Bay' al-Fasid, vol. 6, p. 422) And if he did not witness at the time of taking (correct: at the time of possession), he must become a possessor because it is possession of usurpation (ghasp), and possession of usurpation is possession of guarantee, so it substitutes for possession of sale, which is possession of guarantee. (Al-Binayah: Kitab al-Buyu', Bab al-Bay' al-Fasid, vol. 8, p. 163) Al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Buyu', Bab al-Bay' al-Fasid, vol. 3, p. 57. Usool-e-Hidayah, third part, 57. The author of Hidayah, after mentioning the aforementioned principle, is explaining that if in a two-storey house, one owns the lower floor and the other the upper floor, and both collapse or only the upper collapses, then if he wants to sell only the right of ascent (haqq al-ta'alli) of the upper portion, it is not permissible, because when the upper room collapsed, only the right to climb up remains, and the right of ascent is not wealth because it cannot be preserved, and what is not wealth cannot be sold. Therefore, the right of ascent alone cannot be sold. The right of ascent is not wealth because it is not possible to preserve and collect it, and for sale it is necessary that it be wealth, because sale is the exchange of wealth for wealth. Chapter of Murabahah and Tawliyah. (27) Doubt is like reality in the sale of murabahah, as a precaution. Translation: As a precaution, in murabahah sale, doubt is given the status of reality. (His statement: 'and the upper collapsed') means: the sale of the upper floor after its collapse is not permissible because what remains after its fall is the right of ascent, and it is not wealth because wealth is what can be preserved, and wealth is the sold item itself. (Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: Kitab al-Buyu', Bab al-Bay' al-Fasid, vol. 6, p. 88) He said in al-Fath: When the lower floor belongs to one person and the upper to another, and they fall, or only the upper falls, then the owner of the upper sells his upper floor, it is not permissible because the sold item at that moment is nothing but the right of ascent, and the right of ascent is not wealth because wealth is a substance that can be preserved and held, and it is not a right connected to wealth; rather, it is a right connected to air, and air is not wealth that can be sold. And the sold item must necessarily be one of them. (Radd al-Muhtar: Kitab al-Buyu', Bab al-Bay' al-Fasid, vol. 5, p. 52) Al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Buyu', Bab al-Murabahah wa al-Tawliyah, vol. 3, p. 76.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage from Usool e Hidayah presents two distinct legal principles from the Hanafi school. The first principle (numbered 26) defines wealth (mal) in Islamic law: it is anything that can be preserved and remains over time. This definition is applied to two cases. First, the sale of an absconding slave (al-‘abaq): an absolute runaway cannot be sold due to a hadith prohibiting gharar (uncertainty) and because delivery is impossible. However, if the slave is not a runaway in the buyer's view (e.g., the buyer claims the slave is with him), the sale is valid. The passage also details possession (qabd): if the buyer takes the slave for himself, he becomes a possessor immediately; if he takes it for return to the seller, with a witness it is a trust (amanah) and does not constitute possession of sale, but without a witness it is usurpation (ghasp) and counts as possession in guarantee. The second case involves a two-storey building where the upper floor belongs to one person and the lower to another. If the upper floor collapses, only the right of ascent (haqq al-ta’alli) remains. This right is not wealth because it cannot be preserved or held, so it cannot be sold. The second principle (numbered 27) states: 'Doubt is like reality in murabahah sale, as a precaution.' This means that in a cost-plus sale, if there is doubt about the cost or profit, the precautionary ruling treats the doubt as certainty to avoid potential injustice. The passage references several authorities: Al-Hidayah, Al-‘Inayah, Al-Binayah, Al-Bahr al-Ra’iq, Al-Fath, and Radd al-Muhtar, all confirming the Hanafi position.

Key Terms
الْمَالُ (al-māl): In this text, wealth is defined as that which can be preserved (yumkin iḥrāzuhu) and remains over two time periods.
الْآبِقُ (al-ābiq): An absconding slave; the absolute (muṭlaq) one is a runaway from the owner's perspective without known location.
حَقُّ التَّعَلَّى (ḥaqq al-ta‘allā): The right to climb up (right of ascent) after the upper floor collapses; it is not a property right but a right in the air.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the sale of an absolute absconding slave prohibited in Hanafi fiqh?
A) Because the price is unknown.
B) Because of a hadith prohibiting gharar and the inability to deliver.
C) Because the owner cannot prove ownership.
D) Because the slave is considered a human not property.

Key Takeaway
Wealth in Hanafi fiqh is defined by its ability to be preserved; anything that cannot be preserved, such as a right to climb, cannot be sold.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
6 min
Principle of Shubhah (Doubt) in Murabahah Sale
States the Hanafi principle that doubt is treated as reality in murabahah sales.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that doubt (shubhah) is treated as reality in murabahah sales and explain the precautionary rationale.

Core Idea
In murabahah, doubt about cost is treated as reality to prevent injustice and usury.

The second principle deals with murabahah sale, where the seller discloses the cost and adds a profit. If there is doubt about the exact cost, the Hanafi school treats the doubt as if it were certain, out of precaution (ihtiyatan). This ensures that the transaction is transparent and avoids any element of ribā.

Guided Path
1
Define murabahah sale
Murabahah is a sale where the seller informs the buyer of the cost and adds a known profit.
2
Understand the problem of doubt
If there is uncertainty about the cost, it could lead to dispute or unjust enrichment (ribā).
3
Apply the principle
The doubt is given the status of reality so that the sale is concluded on a clear basis.
4
Note the precautionary nature
The principle is applied ihtiyatan, meaning as a precaution to avoid potential prohibition.
5
Contrast with other sales
In a regular sale, doubt about price might render it fasid, but here the doubt is assumed to be truth for the sake of validity.

(۲۷) اَلشُّبُهَةُ كَالْحَقِيقَةِ فِي بَيْعِ الْمُرَابَحَةِ اِحْتِيَاطَاً.
ترجمہ: احتیاطاً بیع مرابحہ میں شبہ کو حقیقت کا درجہ دے دیا گیا ہے۔
(قَوْلُهُ وَعُلْوِ سَقَطَ أَى لَمْ يَجُزُ بَيْعُ عُلْمٍ بَعْدَ انْهِدَامِهِ لِأَنَّ الْبَاقِيَ بَعْدَ سُقُوطِهِ حَقُّ التَّعَلَّى، وَهُوَ لَيْسَ بِمَالٍ لَّانَّ الْمَالَ مَا يُمْكِنُ إِحْرَازُهُ، وَالْمَالُ هُوَ الْمَحَلُّ الْمَبِيعُ. (البحر الرائق: كتاب البيوع، باب البيع الفاسد، ج ۶ ص ۸۸) قَالَ فِي الْفَتْحِ وَإِذَا كَانَ السُّفُلُ لِرَجُلٍ وَعُلُوهُ لِآخَرَ فَسَقَطَا أَوْ سَقَطَ الْعُلُوُ وَحْدَهُ فَبَاعَ صَاحِبُ الْعُلْوِ عُلُوهُ لَمْ يَجُزُ ، لأنَّ الْمَسِيعَ حِينَئِذٍ لَيْسَ إِلَّا حَقُّ التَّعَلِّى، وَحَقُّ التَّعَلِّى لَيْسَ بِمَالٍ، لِأَنَّ الْمَالَ عَيْنْ يُمْكِنُ إِحْرَاؤُهَا وَإِمْسَاكُهَا وَلَا هُوَ حَقٌّ مُتَعَلِّقٌ بِالْمَالِ بَلْ هُوَ حَقٌّ مُتَعَلِّقٌ بِالْهَوَاءِ، وَلَيْسَ الْهَوَاءُ مَالًا يُبَاعُ وَالْمَسِيعُ لَا بُدَّ أَنْ يَكُونَ أَحَدَهُمَا. (رد المحتار: كتاب البيوع، باب البیع الفاسد، ج ۵ ص ۵۲)
الهداية: كتاب البيوع، باب المرابحة والتولية، ح٣ ص ٧٦
Wealth is what can be preserved. Translation: Wealth is that which can be preserved. 56 Explanation: The meaning of this principle is that, according to Shari'ah, wealth is called that which can remain for two durations and can be preserved, and that which cannot be preserved is not called wealth. The sale of an absconding slave is not permissible. The sale of the absolute absconder is not permissible, because Muhammad mentioned in al-Asl with his statement: It has reached us from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) that he forbade the sale of gharar and the sale of an absconding slave, and because it is not possible to deliver. And the absconding slave that is not absolute, i.e., he is not an absconder in the right of one of the two contracting parties, his sale is permissible, like one who sells him to a man who claims that the slave is with him, because what is prohibited is the sale of the absolute one, and this is not an absconder in the right of the buyer, so the inability to deliver, which prevents permissibility, is negated. Then: Does he become a possessor merely by the contract or not? If he took possession for himself, he becomes a possessor immediately after purchase by agreement. If he took possession for return, then either he witnesses that or not. If he witnesses, he does not become a possessor because it is a trust (amanah) with him, such that if it perishes before reaching the owner, it perishes from the owner's wealth, and possession of a trust does not substitute for possession of sale because possession of guarantee is stronger due to its being confirmed by obligation and ownership. As for obligation, if the buyer refrains from taking possession of the sold item, he is compelled to do so, and after possession the seller cannot rescind. (Al-'Inayah: Kitab al-Buyu', Bab al-Bay' al-Fasid, vol. 6, p. 422) And if he did not witness at the time of taking (correct: at the time of possession), he must become a possessor because it is possession of usurpation (ghasp), and possession of usurpation is possession of guarantee, so it substitutes for possession of sale, which is possession of guarantee. (Al-Binayah: Kitab al-Buyu', Bab al-Bay' al-Fasid, vol. 8, p. 163) Al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Buyu', Bab al-Bay' al-Fasid, vol. 3, p. 57. Usool-e-Hidayah, third part, 57. The author of Hidayah, after mentioning the aforementioned principle, is explaining that if in a two-storey house, one owns the lower floor and the other the upper floor, and both collapse or only the upper collapses, then if he wants to sell only the right of ascent (haqq al-ta'alli) of the upper portion, it is not permissible, because when the upper room collapsed, only the right to climb up remains, and the right of ascent is not wealth because it cannot be preserved, and what is not wealth cannot be sold. Therefore, the right of ascent alone cannot be sold. The right of ascent is not wealth because it is not possible to preserve and collect it, and for sale it is necessary that it be wealth, because sale is the exchange of wealth for wealth. Chapter of Murabahah and Tawliyah. (27) Doubt is like reality in the sale of murabahah, as a precaution. Translation: As a precaution, in murabahah sale, doubt is given the status of reality. (His statement: 'and the upper collapsed') means: the sale of the upper floor after its collapse is not permissible because what remains after its fall is the right of ascent, and it is not wealth because wealth is what can be preserved, and wealth is the sold item itself. (Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: Kitab al-Buyu', Bab al-Bay' al-Fasid, vol. 6, p. 88) He said in al-Fath: When the lower floor belongs to one person and the upper to another, and they fall, or only the upper falls, then the owner of the upper sells his upper floor, it is not permissible because the sold item at that moment is nothing but the right of ascent, and the right of ascent is not wealth because wealth is a substance that can be preserved and held, and it is not a right connected to wealth; rather, it is a right connected to air, and air is not wealth that can be sold. And the sold item must necessarily be one of them. (Radd al-Muhtar: Kitab al-Buyu', Bab al-Bay' al-Fasid, vol. 5, p. 52) Al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Buyu', Bab al-Murabahah wa al-Tawliyah, vol. 3, p. 76.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage from Usool e Hidayah presents two distinct legal principles from the Hanafi school. The first principle (numbered 26) defines wealth (mal) in Islamic law: it is anything that can be preserved and remains over time. This definition is applied to two cases. First, the sale of an absconding slave (al-‘abaq): an absolute runaway cannot be sold due to a hadith prohibiting gharar (uncertainty) and because delivery is impossible. However, if the slave is not a runaway in the buyer's view (e.g., the buyer claims the slave is with him), the sale is valid. The passage also details possession (qabd): if the buyer takes the slave for himself, he becomes a possessor immediately; if he takes it for return to the seller, with a witness it is a trust (amanah) and does not constitute possession of sale, but without a witness it is usurpation (ghasp) and counts as possession in guarantee. The second case involves a two-storey building where the upper floor belongs to one person and the lower to another. If the upper floor collapses, only the right of ascent (haqq al-ta’alli) remains. This right is not wealth because it cannot be preserved or held, so it cannot be sold. The second principle (numbered 27) states: 'Doubt is like reality in murabahah sale, as a precaution.' This means that in a cost-plus sale, if there is doubt about the cost or profit, the precautionary ruling treats the doubt as certainty to avoid potential injustice. The passage references several authorities: Al-Hidayah, Al-‘Inayah, Al-Binayah, Al-Bahr al-Ra’iq, Al-Fath, and Radd al-Muhtar, all confirming the Hanafi position.

Key Terms
الشُّبْهَةُ (al-shubhah): Doubt or uncertainty about a fact, e.g., the exact cost in a murabahah sale.
الْمُرَابَحَةُ (al-murābaḥah): A sale where the seller discloses the cost and adds a known profit margin.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is doubt treated as reality in murabahah sales?
A) To encourage speculation.
B) As a precaution to avoid injustice and ribā.
C) Because the cost is never knowable.
D) To simplify accounting.

Key Takeaway
In murabahah, doubt about the cost is treated as reality to ensure the sale is valid and free from usury.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Sale of Right of Elevation is Invalid
Ruling on the sale of right of elevation with proof from definition of mal and sale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling the right of elevation after building collapse and explain why it is not considered property (mal).

Core Idea
The right of elevation is not property—since it cannot be possessed, it cannot be sold.

In this passage, the author of al-Hidayah addresses a specific scenario: a two-storey building where two different people own the ground and upper floors. After the upper floor collapses, the owner retains only the right to rebuild—the right of elevation. Can this right be sold? The Hanafi school answers with a clear no, and the reasoning reveals a fundamental principle of what constitutes property (mal) in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Understand the building ownership structure: one person owns the lower floor, another the upper. The upper collapses. What remains?
2
Define the right of elevation
Recognize that the owner of the now-destroyed upper floor has haqq al-ta'la—the right to climb and use the airspace for reconstruction.
3
Apply the definition of mal
Recall that in Hanafi fiqh, mal is a physical entity (ayn) that can be acquired and stored. Haqq al-ta'la is intangible and cannot be stored.
The passage explicitly quotes: 'al-mal ma yumkin ihrazuhu'.
4
Apply the definition of sale
Sale (bay') is defined as exchange of property for property. Since haqq al-ta'la is not mal, selling it is invalid.
The dalil: 'لأن البيع اسم لمبادلة المال بالمال'.
5
Conclude the ruling
Thus, the sale of haqq al-ta'la alone is not permissible (la yajuz). The sale would be fasid (void).
This is consistent across the Hanafi sources (al-Hidayah, al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, Radd al-Muhtar).

Source Text

(البحر الرائق: كتاب البيوع، باب البيع الفاسد، ج ۶ ص ۸۸) قَالَ فِي الْفَتْحِ وَإِذَا كَانَ السُّفُلُ لِرَجُلٍ وَعُلُوهُ لِآخَرَ فَسَقَطَا أَوْ سَقَطَ الْعُلُوُ وَحْدَهُ فَبَاعَ صَاحِبُ الْعُلْوِ عُلُوهُ لَمْ يَجُزُ ، لأنَّ الْمَسِيعَ حِينَئِذٍ لَيْسَ إِلَّا حَقُّ التَّعَلِّى، وَحَقُّ التَّعَلِّى لَيْسَ بِمَالٍ، لِأَنَّ الْمَالَ عَيْنْ يُمْكِنُ إِحْرَاؤُهَا وَإِمْسَاكُهَا وَلَا هُوَ حَقٌّ مُتَعَلِّقٌ بِالْمَالِ بَلْ هُوَ حَقٌّ مُتَعَلِّقٌ بِالْهَوَاءِ، وَلَيْسَ الْهَوَاءُ مَالًا يُبَاعُ وَالْمَسِيعُ لَا بُدَّ أَنْ يَكُونَ أَحَدَهُمَا. The author of al-Hidayah, after mentioning the principle, is explaining that if in a two-storey building one person owns the ground floor and another the upper floor, and both collapse or only the upper floor collapses, then if the owner of the upper floor wants to sell the right of elevation (haqq al-ta'la), it is not permissible. Because when the upper floor collapsed, only the right of climbing remains, and the right of climbing is not property (mal), since it cannot be preserved. And what is not property cannot be sold. Therefore, the right of elevation cannot be sold alone. The right of elevation is not property because it cannot be acquired and stored, and for a sale it is necessary that the object be property. Translation: As a precaution, doubt is given the status of reality in a murabahah sale.) (His saying: 'and the upper part collapsed' means that it is not permissible to sell the upper part after its demolition because what remains after its fall is the right of elevation, and it is not property, for property is that which can be acquired, and property is the subject matter of sale. (al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, Kitab al-Buyu', Bab al-Bay' al-Fasid, vol. 6, p. 88) He said in al-Fath: 'When the lower floor belongs to a man and the upper floor to another, and they collapse or only the upper floor collapses, and the owner of the upper sells his upper floor, it is not permissible, because the object sold then is nothing but the right of elevation, and the right of elevation is not property. For property is a physical entity that can be acquired and held, and it is not a right pertaining to property, but rather a right pertaining to air. And air is not property that can be sold. The object sold must necessarily be one of them (i.e., property or a right pertaining to property). (Radd al-Muhtar, Kitab al-Buyu', Bab al-Bay' al-Fasid, vol. 5, p. 52) Al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Buyu', Bab al-Murabahah wa al-Tawliyah, vol. 3, p. 76.)
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the Hanafi school regarding the sale of a right of elevation (haqq al-ta'la) after the upper floor of a building has collapsed. The author of al-Hidayah explains that if two people own different levels of a building—one the lower floor, the other the upper floor—and the upper collapses, the owner of the upper only retains the right to use the airspace above the lower floor for reconstruction. This right of elevation is not considered property (mal) in Hanafi law because it cannot be physically possessed, stored, or secured. Since sale (bay') is defined as the exchange of property for property, a non-property right cannot be sold. The passage cites supporting texts from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, Fath al-Qadir, and Radd al-Muhtar, all confirming that the right of elevation is merely a right related to air (haqq muta'alliq bi al-hawa'), not a tangible asset, and thus unsaleable. The ruling is consistent with the Hanafi principle that only property (mal) can be the subject of a valid sale.

Key Terms
حق التعلّى (haqq al-ta'la): The right to climb or use the airspace above a lower floor after the upper floor has collapsed; a right of elevation.
مال (mal): Property: a physical entity (ayn) that can be acquired and stored (yumkin ihrazuha wa imsakuha).
بيع (bay'): Sale: exchange of property for property (mubadala al-mal bi al-mal).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the sale of the right of elevation (haqq al-ta'la) not permissible according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because the building owner did not consent
B) Because it is not considered property (mal) as it cannot be physically possessed or stored
C) Because the right of elevation is a servitude that expires with the building
D) Because Hanafi law prohibits all sales of airspace

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, only property (mal) that can be physically possessed may be sold; the right of elevation is not mal, so its sale is invalid.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Doubt equals reality in Murabahah sales
Application of the principle that doubt of reduction equals actual reduction in Murabahah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that doubt of price reduction in a Murabahah sale is treated as actual reduction, and identify when a Murabahah sale is impermissible due to such doubt.

Core Idea
In Murabahah, doubt of reduction equals actual reduction: trust is violated if cost is uncertain.

Murabahah is a sale based on trust—the seller must honestly disclose the original cost. But what if he acquired the item through a compromise (sulh) where the actual price may have been less? The Hanafi school rules that any doubt about a reduction is treated as an actual reduction, making the Murabahah sale impermissible. This lesson unpacks that rule and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? A Murabahah sale where the seller acquired the item through sulh. The ruling: it is impermissible (la yajuz) because of doubt about the cost.
The act is the Murabahah sale, not the sulh itself.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The proof is rational (dalil aqli): Murabahah is based on amanah (trust). Any doubt about the original price undermines that trust. The authors cite no specific textual evidence but rely on the principle of precaution (ihtiyat).
The proof is not from Quran or Sunnah directly, but from the nature of the contract.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does doubt equate to reality? Since trust is the foundation, the mere possibility of reduction is treated as actual reduction to avoid deceit. This is a precautionary measure specific to Murabahah.
This is not a general rule; it applies only where the contract requires disclosure of cost.
4
Note the ikhtilaf (disagreement)
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may allow the Murabahah sale if the seller discloses the doubt or the method of acquisition. The Hanafi school strictly prohibits it without such disclosure, but even with disclosure, the doubt remains and the sale is impermissible.
The disagreement is about the weight given to doubt vs. disclosure.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi (relied upon) and is consistently upheld in Zahir al-Riwayah texts. There is no internal Hanafi disagreement.
The principle is authoritative and not overridden by istihsan.

Source Text

باب المرابحة والتولية (۲۷) اَلشُّبُهَةُ كَالْحَقِيقَةِ فِي بَيْعِ الْمُرَابَحَةِ اِحْتِيَاطَاً. ترجمہ: احتیاطاً بیع مرابحہ میں شبہ کو حقیقت کا درجہ دے دیا گیا ہے۔ (قَوْلُهُ وَعُلْوِ سَقَطَ أَى لَمْ يَجُزُ بَيْعُ عُلْمٍ بَعْدَ انْهِدَامِهِ لِأَنَّ الْبَاقِيَ بَعْدَ سُقُوطِهِ حَقُّ التَّعَلَّى، وَهُوَ لَيْسَ بِمَالٍ لَّانَّ الْمَالَ مَا يُمْكِنُ إِحْرَازُهُ، وَالْمَالُ هُوَ الْمَحَلُّ الْمَبِيعُ. (البحر الرائق: كتاب البيوع، باب البيع الفاسد، ج ۶ ص ۸۸) قَالَ فِي الْفَتْحِ وَإِذَا كَانَ السُّفُلُ لِرَجُلٍ وَعُلُوهُ لِآخَرَ فَسَقَطَا أَوْ سَقَطَ الْعُلُوُ وَحْدَهُ فَبَاعَ صَاحِبُ الْعُلْوِ عُلُوهُ لَمْ يَجُزُ ، لأنَّ الْمَسِيعَ حِينَئِذٍ لَيْسَ إِلَّا حَقُّ التَّعَلِّى، وَحَقُّ التَّعَلِّى لَيْسَ بِمَالٍ، لِأَنَّ الْمَالَ عَيْنْ يُمْكِنُ إِحْرَاؤُهَا وَإِمْسَاكُهَا وَلَا هُوَ حَقٌّ مُتَعَلِّقٌ بِالْمَالِ بَلْ هُوَ حَقٌّ مُتَعَلِّقٌ بِالْهَوَاءِ، وَلَيْسَ الْهَوَاءُ مَالًا يُبَاعُ وَالْمَسِيعُ لَا بُدَّ أَنْ يَكُونَ أَحَدَهُمَا. (رد المحتار: كتاب البيوع، باب البیع الفاسد، ج ۵ ص ۵۲) الهداية: كتاب البيوع، باب المرابحة والتولية، ح٣ ص ٧٦

اصول بدایہ ثالث
۵۸
تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ بیع مرابحہ میں اگر ثمن کی کمی کا شبہ ہو تو یہ عدم جواز میں حقیقت میں کمی کی طرح ہے، کیونکہ بیع مرابحہ کی بنیاد امانت پر ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ بیع مرابحہ اس چیز میں درست نہیں ہے جو بطور صلح کی ہو، مثلاً ایک آدمی نے دوسرے پر دس دراہم کا دعوی کیا ، تو مدعی علیہ نے بطور صلح مدعی کو ایک کپڑا دے دیا، اب یقینی طور پر اُس کی قیمت کا علم نہیں ہے، تو مدعی کے لئے جائز نہیں ہے کہ وہ یہ کہے کہ یہ کپڑا میں نے دس دراہم میں لیا ہے اور پندرہ میں آپ پر فروخت کرتا ہوں، کیونکہ مذکورہ صورت میں ثمن اول میں کمی کا شبہ ہے اور بیع مرابحہ میں شبہ حقیقت کی مانند ہے، لہذا مذکورہ صورت میں شبہ کی وجہ سے بیع مرابحہ جائز نہ ہوگی۔ بیع میں مقصود ثمن کی مکمل وصول یابی ہوتی ہے، جبکہ صلح معافی اور درگزر پر مبنی ہوتی ہے، صلح میں مقصود صرف نزاع ختم کرنا ہوتا ہے اور اس میں عموماً مکمل رقم کی ادائیگی نہیں ہوتی، جبکہ بیع مرابحہ کی بنیاد امانت پر ہوتی ہے، اس لیے اس میں شبہ حقیقت کی مانند ہوتا ہے۔ بِخِلافِ مَا إِذَا صَالَحَهُ عَلَى هَذِهِ الْجَارِيَةِ صُلْحًا، فَإِنَّهُ لَا يَبِيعُهَا مُرَابَحَةً حَتَّى يُبَيِّنَ، لأَنَّ الشِّرَاءَ مَبْنِيٌّ عَلَى الاسْتِقْصَاءِ، وَالصُّلْحُ مَبْنِيٌّ عَلَى التَّجَاوُزِ بِدُونِ الْحَقِّ فَعِنْدَ ذَلِكَ لَفظ الصُّلح يُمْكِنُ شُبُهَةُ الْحَطِيطَةِ، وَالشُّبُهَةُ كَالْحَقِيقَةِ فِي الْمَنْعِ مِنْ بَيْعِ الْمُرَابَحَةِ، لِأَنَّهُ مَبْنِيٌّ عَلَى الْأَمَانَة. (المبسوط للسرخسي: كتاب المضاربة، باب المرابحة في المضاربة، ج ۲۲ ص ۷۵) فإنها كحقيقتها احتياطا في بيع المرابحة، ولهذا لو كان لرجل على آخر عشرة دراهم فصالحه منها على ثوب لا يبيع الثوب مرابحة على العشرة لأن الصلح مبناه على التجوز والحطيطة، ولو وجد الحط حقيقة ما جاز البيع مرابحة. (العناية: كتاب البيوع، باب المرابحة والتولية، ج ۶ ص ۵۰۲)
Section: al-Murabahah and al-Tawliyah. (27) The doubt is like the reality in the sale of murabahah as a precaution. Translation: As a precaution, in a murabahah sale, doubt is given the status of reality. (His saying: 'and the upper part collapsed' means that it is not permissible to sell the upper part after its demolition because what remains after its fall is the right of elevation, and it is not property, for property is that which can be acquired, and property is the subject matter of sale. (al-Bahr al-Ra'iq...) He said in al-Fath: ... (Radd al-Muhtar...) Al-Hidayah: ... Explanation: The meaning of the aforementioned principle is that in a murabahah sale, if there is doubt about a reduction in the price (thaman), this is like an actual reduction in terms of impermissibility, because the murabahah sale is based on trust. The author of al-Hidayah, after mentioning the principle, is explaining that a murabahah sale is not valid for something acquired by way of sulh (compromise). For example, a man claimed ten dirhams from another, and the defendant gave a garment to the claimant by way of sulh. Now the exact price of the garment is not known with certainty. So it is not permissible for the claimant to say: 'I bought this garment for ten dirhams and sell it to you for fifteen,' because in this case, there is doubt about a reduction in the first price, and in a murabahah sale, doubt is like reality. Therefore, in this case, the murabahah sale will not be valid due to doubt. In a sale, the objective is full receipt of the price, whereas sulh is based on forgiveness and forbearance. In sulh, the objective is merely to end the dispute, and generally, the full amount is not paid. Whereas a murabahah sale is based on trust, so doubt in it is like reality. Contrary to the case where he makes a sulh with him regarding this slave-girl, then he does not sell her by murabahah until he discloses (the details), because purchase is based on exacting (the full price), while sulh is based on forgoing part of the right. Thus, in that case, the word 'sulh' can create a suspicion of reduction, and doubt is like reality in prohibiting a murabahah sale, because it is based on trust. (al-Mabsut li al-Sarakhsi: Kitab al-Mudarabah, Bab al-Murabahah fi al-Mudarabah, vol. 22, p. 75) Indeed, it is like its reality as a precaution in a murabahah sale. For this reason, if a man has ten dirhams owed by another, and he settles (sulh) with him for a garment, he does not sell the garment by murabahah on the basis of ten, because sulh is based on concession and reduction. If a reduction were found in reality, the murabahah sale would not be permissible. (al-'Inayah: Kitab al-Buyu', Bab al-Murabahah wa al-Tawliyah, vol. 6, p. 502)
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage elaborates on the second principle (number 27) of al-Hidayah's section on Murabahah and Tawliyah sales: 'Doubt (shubhah) is treated as reality (haqiqah) in a Murabahah sale, as a precaution.' The commentary explains the rationale: a Murabahah sale is a trust-based transaction where the seller must disclose the original cost price. If there is any suspicion that the seller might have obtained the item at a lower price than what he claims (e.g., through sulh, where concessions are common), then the Murabahah sale is not permissible because the trust is compromised. The example given: a claimant accepts a garment as sulh (compromise) for a debt of ten dirhams. Since sulh often involves forgoing part of the debt, the actual cost of the garment to the claimant is uncertain—it might have been less than ten. Thus, he cannot sell it by Murabahah claiming the cost price as ten, because the doubt of reduction is equivalent to an actual reduction. The passage contrasts sulh with a regular purchase (shira'), where the buyer pays the full price, so no such doubt arises. The Hanafi scholars (al-Sarakhsi, al-'Inayah) confirm that this principle is unique to Murabahah due to its amanah (trust) basis.

Key Terms
شبهة (shubhah): Doubt or suspicion about a fact that could affect the validity of a Murabahah sale, particularly whether the seller acquired the item at a lower price.
بييع مرابحة (bay' al-murabahah): A sale where the seller discloses the original cost and adds a known profit margin; it is a trust-based sale.
صلح (sulh): A compromise or settlement of a dispute, often involving forgoing part of a claim; usually ends the dispute without full payment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person accepts a garment as sulh (compromise) for a debt of 10 dirhams. Can he sell the garment by Murabahah claiming the cost price as 10 dirhams?
A) Yes, because the debt was 10 dirhams
B) No, because sulh may involve a reduction, so there is doubt about the actual cost
C) Yes, but he must disclose that it was acquired by sulh
D) No, because sulh is a different contract type than sale

Key Takeaway
In a Murabahah sale, any doubt about a reduction in the original cost is treated as an actual reduction, making the sale impermissible to preserve the trust basis of the contract.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Suspicion of Reduction in Murabahah Treated as Actual Reduction
Explains that in murabahah, suspicion of price reduction is treated as actual reduction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that suspicion of a price reduction in a murabahah sale is treated like an actual reduction, and apply it to cases involving sulh settlements.

Core Idea
In murabahah, suspicion of a reduction in the original price is treated as reality, because the contract is based on trust.

This lesson explores a key Hanafi principle governing murabahah (trust-based cost-plus sale): any doubt about a reduction in the original price is legally equivalent to an actual reduction. The consequence is that such a sale becomes impermissible without full disclosure. We will examine the textual basis, the example of a sulh settlement, and the rationale rooted in the trust nature of murabahah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage rules on a murabahah sale where there is suspicion that the first price was reduced. The ruling: such a sale is not permissible (la yajuz).
The term 'ja'iz nahi' indicates impermissibility, which in Hanafi fiqh may be makruh tahrimi or fasid.
2
Locate the key principle
The governing principle is 'shubhah ka al-haqiqah' — suspicion is like reality. This applies specifically in murabahah due to its trust basis.
This principle is not universal; it applies to trust-based contracts (bai' al-amanah).
3
Analyze the example
A person claims ten dirhams from another, settles for a cloth (sulh). That cloth cannot be sold as murabahah claiming it was bought for ten dirhams, because the settlement likely involved a concession and the actual price is uncertain.
The sulh inherently involves potential reduction, creating suspicion.
4
Understand the rationale
Murabahah is built on trust (amanah); the seller must be transparent about the cost. Sulh is built on forgiveness (tajawuz) and ending disputes, often without full recovery. Hence, when the item originates from sulh, the trust is compromised.
The text cites al-Mabsut and al-Inayah to support the principle.
5
Apply the principle
If an item is acquired through any transaction that might have involved a reduction in the price, the seller must disclose this when selling as murabahah; otherwise, the sale is invalid.
The same rule applies to gifts, discounts, or any concession in the original acquisition.

Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that in a murabahah sale, if there is suspicion of a reduction in the price, then in terms of impermissibility it is like an actual reduction, because the murabahah sale is based on trust. The author of al-Hidayah, after mentioning the principle, is explaining that the murabahah sale is not valid for something that was taken by way of sulh (compromise). For example, a person claimed ten dirhams from another, and the defendant gave a cloth to the claimant as a compromise. Now the price of that cloth is not known with certainty. It is not permissible for the claimant to say, 'I bought this cloth for ten dirhams and I am selling it to you for fifteen,' because in this case there is suspicion of a reduction in the first price, and in a murabahah sale suspicion is like reality. Therefore, in the mentioned case, because of the suspicion, the murabahah sale will not be permissible. In a sale, the objective is complete receipt of the price, whereas sulh is based on forgiveness and forbearance. In sulh, the objective is only to end the dispute, and generally the full amount is not paid. On the other hand, the murabahah sale is based on trust, so here suspicion is like reality. Contrary to the case when he makes a compromise with him regarding this slave-girl as a sulh, then he should not sell her as murabahah until he clarifies, because purchase is based on exactingness, and sulh is based on passing over without full right. So in that case the word 'sulh' makes possible a suspicion of reduction, and suspicion is like reality in prohibiting the murabahah sale, because it is based on trust. (Al-Mabsut by al-Sarakhsi: Kitab al-Mudarabah, Bab al-Murabahah fi al-Mudarabah, vol. 22, p. 75) For it (suspicion) is like reality as a precaution in murabahah sale. Hence, if a man had ten dirhams due from another and he compromised with him for a garment, he should not sell the garment as murabahah on the ten, because compromise is based on concession and reduction. If reduction had actually occurred, the murabahah sale would not be permissible. (Al-Inayah: Kitab al-Buyu', Bab al-Murabahah wa al-Tawliyah, vol. 6, p. 502)
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the Hanafi school explaining a key principle in murabahah transactions. The author begins by stating the principle: suspicion of a reduction in the original price is treated as an actual reduction in the context of murabahah. The reason is that murabahah is a trust-based contract where the seller must disclose the original cost. Any doubt about the cost undermines the trust. The text then provides an example: a person claims ten dirhams from another, and the defendant settles by giving a cloth. The claimant cannot later sell that cloth as murabahah claiming he bought it for ten dirhams, because the settlement (sulh) inherently involves a concession, so the actual price is uncertain. The author quotes al-Mabsut and al-Inayah to support that suspicion in murabahah is like reality (shubhah ka al-haqiqah). The principle applies to all cases where the original acquisition involved a compromise or any transaction that may have involved a reduction.

Key Terms
مرابحہ (murabahah): A sale where the seller discloses the original cost and adds a known profit.
صلح (sulh): A compromise or settlement to end a dispute, often involving concession on the owed amount.
شبہ (shubhah): Suspicion or doubt about a matter; in this context, doubt about the actual cost of the item in murabahah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi principle discussed in this lesson, if a person acquires an item through sulh (compromise) and wishes to sell it as murabahah, what must he do?
A) He can sell it as murabahah without disclosure because sulh is a valid contract.
B) He cannot sell it as murabahah at all because sulh always involves reduction.
C) He must disclose the true cost and the fact that it was acquired through sulh; otherwise the murabahah sale is not permissible.
D) He can sell it as murabahah only if he sells it for the same amount as the original claim.

Key Takeaway
In murabahah, any suspicion of a reduction in the original price is treated as actual reduction, making the sale impermissible without full disclosure.

Customary Practice Equals a Stipulated Condition
Legal maxim and its application to murabahah disclosure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle 'al-ma'rufu ka al-mashruti' to determine when disclosure of custom in a murabahah sale is obligatory.

Core Idea
Custom among merchants is like a condition: must disclose any customary practice affecting price.

In Hanafi fiqh, a condition doesn't need to be spoken to be binding—if it's well-known in the market, it has legal force. This lesson explores the principle 'al-ma'rufu ka al-mashruti' through a concrete murabahah example. Understanding this principle is essential for anyone involved in trade or Islamic finance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule
State the principle al-ma'rufu ka al-mashruti in your own words. What does it equate custom to?
The principle is a legal maxim: what is customary is like a condition.
2
Understand the murabahah context
Murabahah is a cost-plus sale requiring full transparency. Any factor that affects the price must be disclosed. Identify why custom matters here.
Installment payment usually implies a higher price, so nondisclosure would break trust.
3
Apply to the case
In the example: no explicit installment term, but local custom is to pay in installments. Explain why disclosure becomes wajib.
Custom makes the term 'as if stipulated' because both parties would assume it.
4
Contrast with other schools
Note that this is a mukhtalaf fih issue—other schools may not require disclosure of unstated custom. Evaluate the Hanafi emphasis on transparency.
Hanafi position relies on the maxim 'Al-ma'rufu bayna al-tujjar ka al-mashruti baynahum.'

Source Text

ترجمہ: جو چیز معروف ہو وہ مشروط کی طرح ہے۔
۵۹
تشریح مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ جو شے لوگوں میں معروف اور مشہور ہو وہ ایسی ہے جیسے کہ اس کی شرط لگائی ہو ، اگر چہ حقیقت میں شرط نہ لگائی ہو۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ اقلا ر ہے ہیں کہ اگر بائع اول اور مشتری اول کے درمیان اجل مقرر نہ ہو، اب اگر مشتری اس کو مرابحہ کے ساتھ فروخت کرے گا تو مشتری پر اس کی وضاحت ضروری ہوگی، کیونکہ مذکورہ بالا صورت میں اگر چہ مشتری اور بائع اول کے درمیان اجل مقر ر نہ تھی لیکن جب مشتری کے شہر وغیرہ میں یہ معروف ہو کہ لوگ شمن مقسطوں پر ادا کرتے ہیں اور چونکہ قسطوں پر شے گراں قیمت پر فروخت کی جاتی ہے، جب یہ معروف ہے تو بمنزلہ مشروط کے ہوا، گویا کہ مشتری اول اور بائع کے درمیان اجل مشروط ہے اور جب اجل مشروط ہو تو مشتری اول پر اس کا بیان کرنا ضروری ہوتا ہے، لہذ امذکورہ صورت میں بھی مشتری پر وضاحت ضروری ہوگی ، اس لیے کہ المعروف بين التجار كالمشروط بينهم يعنى تاجروں کے مابین مشہور ومعروف چیز مشروط کے درجے میں ہوتی ہے، اور مشروط اثمن میں مشتری پر وضاحت لازم ہے، تو معروف کی صورت میں بھی وضاحت ضروری ہوگی۔
الهداية كتاب البيوع، باب المرابحة والتولية، ج ۳ ص۷۸ ولو لم يكن الأجل مشروطا في العقد ولكنه منجم معتاد) كعادة بعض البلدان يشترون بعقد ويسلمون الثمن بعد أشهر، إما جملة وإما منجما قيل: لا بد من بيانه لأن المعروف كالمشروط) أى لأن الثابت بالعرف كالمشروط بين العاقدين. (البناية: كتاب البيوع، باب المرابحة والتولية، ج ۸ ص (۲۴۵) ولو لم يكن الأجل مشروطا في العقد لكنه منجم) معتاد كعادة بعض البلاد يشترون
Principles of Hidayah, Third. (28) Al-Ma'rufu ka al-Mashruti. Translation: Something that is customary is like a condition. 59. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that something that is customary and well-known among the people is as if a condition was stipulated, even if actually no condition was stipulated. The author of al-Hidayah, after mentioning the principle, states that if there is no fixed term (ajal) agreed between the first seller and the first buyer, and then the first buyer sells it as murabahah, then clarification of the term is obligatory upon the buyer. Because even though no term was agreed between the buyer and the first seller, if it is customary in the buyer's city that people pay the price in installments, and since goods sold in installments are sold at a higher price, when this is customary it becomes like a condition. So it is as if a term was agreed between the first buyer and the seller. And if a term is stipulated, it is necessary for the first buyer to disclose it. Therefore in this case also, clarification is obligatory. Because 'Al-ma'rufu bayna al-tujjar ka al-mashruti baynahum' meaning that what is well-known among merchants is in the position of a condition. And when a condition affects the price, disclosure is obligatory for the buyer. So in the case of custom, disclosure is also obligatory. Al-Hidayah, Kitab al-Buyu', Bab al-Murabahah wa al-Tawliyah, vol. 3, p. 78: 'And if the term was not stipulated in the contract but it is customary to pay in installments, like the custom of some countries where they buy by contract and pay the price after months, either in a lump sum or in installments, it is said: disclosure is necessary because custom is like a condition.' i.e., because what is established by custom is like a condition between the contracting parties. (Al-Binayah: Kitab al-Buyu', Bab al-Murabahah wa al-Tawliyah, vol. 8, p. 245) 'And if the term was not stipulated in the contract but it is customary to pay in installments...'
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage presents another key principle from Hanafi fiqh: al-ma'rufu ka al-mashruti (customary practice is like a stipulated condition). The teacher explains that if no payment term is explicitly agreed upon, but it is customary in the local market to pay in installments, then in a murabahah sale the seller must disclose this customary practice. The reason is that custom affects the price, as installment sales often involve a higher price due to credit. Since the murabahah contract is based on trust and requires full disclosure of any factor that affects the cost, the seller must inform the buyer of the customary installment arrangement. The text quotes al-Hidayah and al-Binayah to confirm that this principle is well-established. It applies to any business custom that influences the price or terms of sale.

Key Terms
معروف (ma'ruf): Something that is well-known and customary among people, especially in business transactions.
مشروط (mashrut): A condition explicitly stated in a contract.
اجل (ajal): A fixed term or deadline for payment, whether lump sum or in installments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, if it is customary in a market to pay for goods in installments, and a first buyer later sells the goods as murabahah without mentioning this, what is the ruling?
A) The murabahah sale is valid because no condition was stipulated.
B) The murabahah sale is invalid because the buyer must disclose the customary practice.
C) The murabahah sale is valid only if the first buyer sells at a loss.
D) The murabahah sale is permissible but the buyer may cancel it within three days.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim 'al-ma'rufu ka al-mashruti' obligates disclosure of any customary practice that influences the price in a murabahah sale.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
The Customary is Like the Stipulated: Al-Ma'ruf ka al-Mashrut
Explains the maxim and its application to murabahah disclosure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim al-Ma'ruf ka al-Mashrut and apply it to determine disclosure obligations in murabahah sales.

Core Idea
Custom among merchants has the binding force of an explicit contractual condition.

This lesson introduces one of the most powerful Hanafi legal maxims: 'al-Ma'ruf ka al-Mashrut' — the customary is like the stipulated. While many students think only written or spoken terms matter, Hanafi fiqh recognizes that deeply rooted commercial practices create the same legal obligations as explicit conditions. We will see how this maxim operates in the context of murabahah (cost-plus sale) where a customary installment term must be disclosed even if not agreed upon in words.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim and its literal meaning
Read the opening lines: 'الْمَعْرُوفَ كَالْمَشْرُوطِ' meaning 'the customary is like the stipulated.' Recognize this as a legal maxim (qa'ida kulliya) from the Hanafi school.
The maxim is attributed to Al-Hidayah and is widely applied in commercial transactions.
2
Parse the legal equivalence
Understand that 'al-ma'ruf' refers to well-known, consistent customs among people, especially merchants. 'Al-mashrut' refers to a condition explicitly inserted in a contract. The maxim states that custom creates the same legal obligations as an explicit stipulation.
This is a form of urf (custom) that is binding in Hanafi law.
3
Apply the maxim to the murabahah case
Consider the scenario: a buyer purchases goods without a fixed term but in his locality, installment payment is customary. When he resells on murabahah, he must disclose this custom because it is deemed a stipulated condition. Trace the reasoning: custom = stipulation → disclosure of stipulation is wajib → disclosure of custom is wajib.
The source mentions that the author of Hidayah uses this maxim to deduce the disclosure requirement.
4
Identify the authority and scope
Note that the maxim is cited from Al-Hidayah and Al-Binayah, indicating its strong transmission (zahir al-riwayah). The scope is primarily commercial customs that do not contradict explicit terms.
Other schools may not give equal weight to custom, but Hanafis rely on istihsan and urf.

Source Text

ترجمہ: جو چیز معروف ہو وہ مشروط کی طرح ہے۔

الهداية كتاب البيوع، باب المرابحة والتولية، ج ۳ ص۷۸ ولو لم يكن الأجل مشروطا في العقد ولكنه منجم معتاد) كعادة بعض البلدان يشترون بعقد ويسلمون الثمن بعد أشهر، إما جملة وإما منجما قيل: لا بد من بيانه لأن المعروف كالمشروط) أى لأن الثابت بالعرف كالمشروط بين العاقدين. (البناية: كتاب البيوع، باب المرابحة والتولية، ج ۸ ص (۲۴۵) ولو لم يكن الأجل مشروطا في العقد لكنه منجم) معتاد كعادة بعض البلاد يشترون
Principles of Hidayah, Third (Volume). (28) Al-Ma'ruf ka al-Mashrut. Translation: Whatever is well-known (customary) is like a stipulated condition. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that whatever is well-known and customary among people is as if it were stipulated, even if not actually stipulated. The author of Hidayah, after mentioning this principle, states that if there is no fixed term (ajal) between the first seller and the first buyer, then if the buyer sells it as murabahah, the buyer must disclose it, because in the above case, although there was no fixed term between the buyer and the first seller, when it is customary in the buyer's city etc. that people pay in installments, and since goods are sold at a higher price on installments, when this is customary, it is as if stipulated. Thus, between the first buyer and seller, the term is deemed stipulated, and when a term is stipulated, the first buyer must disclose it. Therefore, in the mentioned case, disclosure is necessary for the buyer, because 'what is customary among merchants is like a stipulated condition among them' means that among merchants, a customary thing has the status of a stipulated condition, and in a stipulated price, disclosure is required, so in the case of custom, disclosure is also necessary. (Al-Hidayah, Kitab al-Buyu', Bab al-Murabahah wa al-Tawliyah, vol. 3, p. 78: 'And if the term was not stipulated in the contract but it is customary to pay in installments, as is the custom of some countries where they buy with a contract and deliver the price after months, either lump sum or in installments, it was said: it is necessary to state it because the customary is like the stipulated' meaning that what is established by custom is like a condition between the contracting parties.) (Al-Binayah: Kitab al-Buyu', Bab al-Murabahah wa al-Tawliyah, vol. 8, p. 245: 'And if the term was not stipulated in the contract but it is customary to pay in installments, as is the custom of some countries where they buy...')
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage introduces and explains the Hanafi legal maxim 'al-Ma'ruf ka al-Mashrut' (the customary is like the stipulated). The author first gives the maxim and its literal translation. Then, in the commentary, he clarifies that anything widely recognized and customary among people takes the legal effect of an explicit condition, even if not verbally stipulated. The author applies this to a specific case in murabahah (cost-plus sale). If the first buyer acquired goods without a fixed payment term, but in his locality the prevalent custom is to pay in installments (and he sells the goods on murabahah basis), he must disclose the fact of the customary installment term because it is as if it had been stipulated. The reason is that custom among merchants is equivalent to a stipulated condition, and where a condition exists regarding the price, disclosure is incumbent. The passage then quotes directly from Al-Hidayah and Al-Binayah to support the principle.

Key Terms
المعروف (al-ma'ruf): The known or customary practice among people, particularly merchants.
المشروط (al-mashrut): A condition explicitly stipulated in a contract.
المرابحة (al-murabahah): A sale where the seller discloses the cost and adds a known profit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a murabahah sale, if the first buyer purchased goods without a fixed payment term but in his city people customarily pay in installments, what is the ruling on the buyer when he resells on murabahah?
A) He does not need to mention the installment custom.
B) He must disclose the customary installment term because custom is like a stipulation.
C) He must disclose only if the term is explicitly mentioned in the first contract.
D) He can choose to disclose or not.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim 'al-Ma'ruf ka al-Mashrut' transforms deeply rooted commercial customs into binding contractual obligations, requiring disclosure in murabahah sales.
